   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Ionization
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <span class="mw-redirectedfrom">
      (Redirected from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Ionisation&amp;redirect=no" title="Ionisation">
       Ionisation
      </a>
      )
     </span>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       This article is about physical processes. For devices, see
       <a href="/wiki/Ion_source" title="Ion source">
        Ion source
       </a>
       . For the musical composition, see
       <a href="/wiki/Ionisation_(Var%C3%A8se)" title="Ionisation (Varèse)">
        Ionisation (Varèse)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <p>
       <b>
        Ionization
       </b>
       (Ionisation), is the process by which an atom or a molecule acquires a negative or positive charge by gaining or losing
       <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
        electrons
       </a>
       to form
       <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">
        ions
       </a>
       , often in conjunction with other chemical changes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ionization can result from the loss of an electron after collisions with
       <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">
        subatomic particles
       </a>
       , collisions with other atoms, molecules and ions, or through the interaction with light.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterolytic_bond_cleavage" title="Heterolytic bond cleavage">
        Heterolytic bond cleavage
       </a>
       and heterolytic
       <a href="/wiki/Substitution_reaction" title="Substitution reaction">
        substitution reactions
       </a>
       can result in the formation of ion pairs. Ionization can occur through radioactive decay by the
       <a href="/wiki/Internal_conversion" title="Internal conversion">
        internal conversion
       </a>
       process, in which an excited nucleus transfers its energy to one of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner-shell_electrons" title="Inner-shell electrons">
        inner-shell electrons
       </a>
       causing it to be ejected.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>
      <div class="toc" id="toc">
       <div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en" xml:lang="en">

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">
        Uses
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Uses">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Everyday examples of gas ionization are such as within a
       <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">
        fluorescent lamp
       </a>
       or other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_discharge" title="Electrical discharge">
        electrical discharge
       </a>
       lamps. It is also used in radiation detectors such as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geiger-M%C3%BCller_counter" title="Geiger-Müller counter">
        Geiger-Müller counter
       </a>
       or the
       <a href="/wiki/Ionization_chamber" title="Ionization chamber">
        ionization chamber
       </a>
       . The ionization process is widely used in a variety of equipment in fundamental science (e.g.,
       <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">
        mass spectrometry
       </a>
       ) and in industry (e.g.,
       <a href="/wiki/Radiation_therapy" title="Radiation therapy">
        radiation therapy
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Production_of_ions">
        Production of ions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Production of ions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electron_avalanche.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1129" data-file-width="1620" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Electron_avalanche.gif/350px-Electron_avalanche.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Electron_avalanche.gif/525px-Electron_avalanche.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Electron_avalanche.gif/700px-Electron_avalanche.gif 2x" width="350"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electron_avalanche.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Avalanche effect between two electrodes. The original ionization event liberates one electron, and each subsequent collision liberates a further electron, so two electrons emerge from each collision: the ionizing electron and the liberated electron.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Negatively charged ions are produced when a free electron collides with an atom and is subsequently trapped inside the electric potential barrier, releasing any excess energy. The process is known as
       <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture_ionization" title="Electron capture ionization">
        electron capture ionization
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Positively charged ions are produced by transferring a sufficient amount of energy to a bound electron in a collision with charged particles (e.g. ions, electrons or positrons) or with photons. The threshold amount of the required energy is known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionization_potential" title="Ionization potential">
        ionization potential
       </a>
       . The study of such collisions is of fundamental importance with regard to the few-body problem (see article on
       <a href="/wiki/Few-body_systems" title="Few-body systems">
        few-body systems
       </a>
       ), which is one of the major unsolved problems in physics.
       <a href="/wiki/Kinematically_complete_experiment" title="Kinematically complete experiment">
        Kinematically complete experiments
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schulz_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-schulz-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       i.e. experiments in which the complete momentum vector of all collision fragments (the scattered projectile, the recoiling target-ion, and the ejected electron) are determined, have contributed to major advances in the theoretical understanding of the few-body problem in recent years.
      </p>
      <p>
       Adiabatic ionization is a form of ionization in which an electron is removed from or added to an
       <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">
        atom
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">
        molecule
       </a>
       in its lowest
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_state" title="Energy state">
        energy state
       </a>
       to form an ion in its lowest energy state.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Townsend_discharge" title="Townsend discharge">
        Townsend discharge
       </a>
       is a good example of the creation of positive ions and free electrons due to ion impact. It is a cascade reaction involving
       <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
        electrons
       </a>
       in a region with a sufficiently high
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">
        electric field
       </a>
       in a gaseous medium that can be ionized, such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">
        air
       </a>
       . Following an original ionization event, due to such as ionizing radiation, the positive
       <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">
        ion
       </a>
       drifts towards the
       <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">
        cathode
       </a>
       , while the free electron drifts towards the
       <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">
        anode
       </a>
       of the device. If the electric field is strong enough, the free electron gains sufficient energy to liberate a further electron when it next collides with another molecule. The two free electrons then travel towards the anode and gain sufficient energy from the electric field to cause impact ionization when the next collisions occur; and so on. This is effectively a chain reaction of electron generation, and is dependent on the free electrons gaining sufficient energy between collisions to sustain the avalanche.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Ionization efficiency is the ratio of the number of ions formed to the number of electrons or photons used.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ionization_energy_of_atoms">
        Ionization energy of atoms
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ionization energy of atoms">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_Ionization_Energy.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="205" data-file-width="512" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/First_Ionization_Energy.svg/500px-First_Ionization_Energy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/First_Ionization_Energy.svg/750px-First_Ionization_Energy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/First_Ionization_Energy.svg/1000px-First_Ionization_Energy.svg.png 2x" width="500"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_Ionization_Energy.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <b>
          Figure 1
         </b>
         . Ionization energies of neutral elements.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The trend in the
       <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">
        ionization energy
       </a>
       of atoms is often used to demonstrate the periodic behavior of atoms with respect to the atomic number, as summarized by ordering atoms in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mendeleev%27s_table" title="Mendeleev's table">
        Mendeleev's table
       </a>
       . This is a valuable tool for establishing and understanding the ordering of electrons in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_orbitals" title="Atomic orbitals">
        atomic orbitals
       </a>
       without going into the details of wave functions or the ionization process. An example is presented in
       <b>
        figure 1
       </b>
       . The periodic abrupt decrease in ionization potential after rare gas atoms, for instance, indicates the emergence of a new shell in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_metals" title="Alkali metals">
        alkali metals
       </a>
       . In addition, the local maximums in the ionization energy plot, moving from left to right in a row, are indicative of s, p, d, and f sub-shells.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Semi-classical_description_of_ionization">
        Semi-classical description of ionization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Semi-classical description of ionization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">
        Classical physics
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">
        Bohr model
       </a>
       of the atom can qualitatively explain
       <a href="/wiki/Photoionization" title="Photoionization">
        photoionization
       </a>
       and collision-mediated ionization. In these cases, during the ionization process, the energy of the electron exceeds the energy difference of the potential barrier it is trying to pass. The semi-classical description, however, cannot describe
       <a href="/wiki/Tunnel_ionization" title="Tunnel ionization">
        tunnel ionization
       </a>
       since the process involves the passage of electron through a classically forbidden potential barrier.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_mechanical_description_of_ionization">
        Quantum mechanical description of ionization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Quantum mechanical description of ionization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The interaction of atoms and molecules with sufficiently strong laser pulses leads to the ionization to singly or multiply charged ions. The ionization rate, i.e. the ionization probability in unit time, can only be calculated using
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">
        quantum mechanics
       </a>
       . In general, the analytic solutions are not available, and the approximations required for manageable numerical calculations do not provide accurate enough results. However, when the laser intensity is sufficiently high, the detailed structure of the atom or molecule can be ignored and analytic solution for the ionization rate is possible.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tunnel_ionization">
        Tunnel ionization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Tunnel ionization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tunnel_ionization_3.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="743" data-file-width="978" height="380" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Tunnel_ionization_3.png/500px-Tunnel_ionization_3.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Tunnel_ionization_3.png/750px-Tunnel_ionization_3.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9b/Tunnel_ionization_3.png 2x" width="500"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tunnel_ionization_3.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Combined potential of an atom and a uniform laser field. At distances
         <span class="nowrap">
          <i>
           r
          </i>
          &lt;
          <i>
           r
          </i>
          <sub>
           0
          </sub>
         </span>
         , the potential of the laser can be neglected, while at distances with
         <span class="texhtml">
          <i>
           r
          </i>
          &gt;
          <i>
           r
          </i>
          <sub>
           0
          </sub>
         </span>
         the Coulomb potential is negligible compared to the potential of the laser field. The electron emerges from under the barrier at
         <span class="texhtml">
          <i>
           r
          </i>
          =
          <i>
           R
          </i>
          <sub>
           c
          </sub>
         </span>
         .
         <span class="texhtml">
          <i>
           E
          </i>
          <sub>
           i
          </sub>
         </span>
         is the ionization potential of the atom.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Tunnel_ionization" title="Tunnel ionization">
        Tunnel ionization
       </a>
       is ionization due to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_tunneling" title="Quantum tunneling">
        quantum tunneling
       </a>
       . In classical ionization, an electron must have enough energy to make it over the potential barrier, but quantum tunneling allows the electron simply to go through the potential barrier instead of going all the way over it because of the wave nature of the electron. The probability of an electron's tunneling through the barrier drops off exponentially with the width of the potential barrier. Therefore, an electron with a higher energy can make it further up the potential barrier, leaving a much thinner barrier to tunnel through and, thus, a greater chance to do so. In practice, tunnel ionization is observable when the atom or molecule is interacting with near-infrared strong laser pulses. This process can be understood as a process by which a bounded electron, through the absorption of more than one photon from the laser field, is ionized. This picture is generally known as multiphoton ionization (MPI).
      </p>
      <p>
       Keldysh
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       modeled the MPI process as a transition of the electron from the ground state of the atom to the Volkov states.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In this model the perturbation of the ground state by the laser field is neglected and the details of atomic structure in determining the ionization probability are not taken into account. The major difficulty with Keldysh's model was its neglect of the effects of Coulomb interaction on the final state of the electron. As it is observed from figure, the Coulomb field is not very small in magnitude compared to the potential of the laser at larger distances from the nucleus. This is in contrast to the approximation made by neglecting the potential of the laser at regions near the nucleus. Perelomov et al.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       included the Coulomb interaction at larger internuclear distances. Their model (which we call PPT model) was derived for short range potential and includes the effect of the long range Coulomb interaction through the first order correction in the quasi-classical action. Larochelle et al.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       have compared the theoretically predicted ion versus intensity curves of rare gas atoms interacting with a Ti:Sapphire laser with experimental measurement. They have shown that the total ionization rate predicted by the PPT model fit very well the experimental ion yields for all rare gases in the intermediate regime of Keldysh parameter.
      </p>
      <p>
       The rate of MPI on atom with an ionization potential

       </span>

      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Quasi-static_tunnel_ionization">
        Quasi-static tunnel ionization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Quasi-static tunnel ionization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The quasi-static tunnelling (QST) is the ionization whose rate can be satisfactorily predicted by the ADK model,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       i.e. the limit of the PPT model when
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \gamma }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              γ
              <!-- γ -->
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \gamma }
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\gamma " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a223c880b0ce3da8f64ee33c4f0010beee400b1a" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.262ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       approaches zero.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SharifiTalebpour2010_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SharifiTalebpour2010-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The rate of QST is given by
      </p>

       <dd>

      <p>
       As compared to
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle W_{PPT}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

               </mi>
               <mi>

               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
            </mstyle>

           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle W_{PPT}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{\displaystyle W_{PPT}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/522eedc13a8322043b30f1077b910640482c8f21" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:6.051ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
       </span>
       the absence of summation over n, which represent different
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Above_threshold_ionization" title="Above threshold ionization">
        above threshold ionization
       </a>
       (ATI) peaks, is remarkable.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Strong_field_approximation_for_the_ionization_rate">
        Strong field approximation for the ionization rate
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Strong field approximation for the ionization rate">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The calculations of PPT are done in the
       <b>
        E
       </b>
       -gauge, meaning that the laser field is taken as electromagnetic waves. The ionization rate can also be calculated in
       <b>
        A
       </b>
       -gauge, which emphasis the particle nature of light (absorbing multiple photons during ionization). This approach was adopted by Krainov model
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krainov1997_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Krainov1997-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       based on the earlier works of Faisal
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faisal1973_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Faisal1973-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and Reiss.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reiss1980_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Reiss1980-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The resulting rate is given by
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle W_{KRA}=\sum _{n=N}^{n=\infty }2\pi \omega ^{2}p\left(n-n_{\mathrm {osc} }\right)^{2}\int \mathrm {d} \Omega \left|FT\left(I_{KAR}\Psi \left(\mathbf {r} \right)\right)\right|^{2}J_{n}^{2}\left(n_{f},{\frac {n_{\mathrm {osc} }}{2}}\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>
                W
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 K
                </mi>
                <mi>
                 R
                </mi>
                <mi>
                 A
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <munderover>
               <mo>
                ∑
                <!-- ∑ -->
               </mo>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 n
                </mi>

                </mo>

                 N
                </mi>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

                </mi>

                </mo>

                 ∞
                 <!-- ∞ -->
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </munderover>
              <mn>
               2
              </mn>
              <mi>
               π
               <!-- π -->
              </mi>

      <h3>
       <span id="Atomic_stabilization.2Fpopulation_trapping">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Atomic_stabilization/population_trapping">
        Atomic stabilization/population trapping
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Atomic stabilization/population trapping">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In calculating the rate of MPI of atoms only transitions to the continuum states are considered. Such an approximation is acceptable as long as there is no multiphoton resonance between the ground state and some excited states. However, in real situation of interaction with pulsed lasers, during the evolution of laser intensity, due to different Stark shift of the ground and excited states there is a possibility that some excited state go into multiphoton resonance with the ground state. Within the dressed atom picture, the ground state dressed by
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle m}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              m
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle m}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="m" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0a07d98bb302f3856cbabc47b2b9016692e3f7bc" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.04ex; height:1.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       photons and the resonant state undergo an avoided crossing at the resonance intensity
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle I_{r}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               I
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                r
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle I_{r}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="I_r" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/540b248dfa84cd32dc16402b1cade5866cd6fd2f" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.997ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
       </span>
       . The minimum distance,
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle V_{m}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               V
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                m
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle V_{m}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="V_{m}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/40836783b20c374654ab03ddd6b01586ad9b4fb8" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.03ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
       </span>
       , at the avoided crossing is proportional to the generalized Rabi frequency,
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \Gamma (t)=\Gamma _{m}I(t)^{m/2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi mathvariant="normal">
              Γ
              <!-- Γ -->
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              (
             </mo>
             <mi>
              t
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              )
             </mo>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <msub>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               Γ
               <!-- Γ -->
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                m
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mi>
              I
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              (
             </mo>
             <mi>
              t
             </mi>
             <msup>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                m
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mo>
                 /
                </mo>
               </mrow>
               <mn>
                2
               </mn>
              </mrow>
             </msup>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \Gamma (t)=\Gamma _{m}I(t)^{m/2}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\Gamma (t)=\Gamma _{m}I(t)^{{m/2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3651b62de0d97202bb9e1c8bbae9db58588ff786" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:17.468ex; height:3.343ex;"/>
       </span>
       coupling the two states. According to Story et al.,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StoryDuncan1994_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-StoryDuncan1994-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the probability of remaining in the ground state,
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle P_{g}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               P
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                g
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle P_{g}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="P_g" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/27be4b7cf2e3935e98019429e156580304189d0b" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:2.514ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       , is given by
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle P_{g}=\exp \left(-{\frac {2\pi W_{m}^{2}}{\mathrm {d} W/\mathrm {d} t}}\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>

               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>

                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               exp
              </mi>
              <mo>
               ⁡
               <!-- ⁡ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow>
               <mo>
                (
               </mo>
               <mrow>
                <mo>
                 −
                 <!-- − -->
                </mo>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mfrac>
                  <mrow>
                   <mn>
                    2
                   </mn>
                   <mi>
                    π
                    <!-- π -->
                   </mi>
                   <msubsup>
                    <mi>
                     W
                    </mi>
                    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                     <mi>
                      m
                     </mi>
                    </mrow>
                    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                     <mn>
                      2
                     </mn>
                    </mrow>
                   </msubsup>
                  </mrow>
                  <mrow>
                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                    <mi mathvariant="normal">

                    </mi>

                   <mi>

                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

                    </mo>

                  </mrow>
                 </mfrac>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
               <mo>

               </mo>
              </mrow>

            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle P_{g}=\exp \left(-{\frac {2\pi W_{m}^{2}}{\mathrm {d} W/\mathrm {d} t}}\right)}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\displaystyle P_{g}=\exp \left(-{\frac {2\pi W_{m}^{2}}{\mathrm {d} W/\mathrm {d} t}}\right)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0c145c3413f87e3347ea19651c87824fd04d1cb9" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:22.252ex; height:6.343ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle W}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              W
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle W}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="W" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/54a9c4c547f4d6111f81946cad242b18298d70b7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.435ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the time-dependent energy difference between the two dressed states. In interaction with a short pulse, if the dynamic resonance is reached in the rising or the falling part of the pulse, the population practically remains in the ground state and the effect of multiphoton resonances may be neglected. However, if the states go onto resonance at the peak of the pulse, where
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathrm {d} W/\mathrm {d} t=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               d
              </mi>
             </mrow>
             <mi>
              W
             </mi>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mo>
               /
              </mo>
             </mrow>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               d
              </mi>
             </mrow>
             <mi>
              t
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              0
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \mathrm {d} W/\mathrm {d} t=0}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{\displaystyle \mathrm {d} W/\mathrm {d} t=0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/02e737118c56f19ff104c4fd53e6f27764c7160b" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.283ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       , then the excited state is populated. After being populated, since the ionization potential of the excited state is small, it is expected that the electron will be instantly ionized.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1992, de Boer and Muller
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       showed that Xe atoms subjected to short laser pulses could survive in the highly excited states 4f, 5f, and 6f . These states were believed to have been excited by the dynamic Stark shift of the levels into multiphoton resonance with the field during the rising part of the laser pulse. Subsequent evolution of the laser pulse did not ionize completely these states leaving behind some highly excited atoms. We shall refer to this phenomenon as "population trapping".
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lambda_type_population_trapping.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="597" height="440" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Lambda_type_population_trapping.png/500px-Lambda_type_population_trapping.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6b/Lambda_type_population_trapping.png 1.5x" width="500"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lambda_type_population_trapping.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The schematic presentation of lambda type population trapping. G is the ground state of the atom. 1 and 2 are two degenerate excited states. After the population is transferred to the states due to multiphoton resonance, these states are coupled through continuum c and the population is trapped in the superposition of these states.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       We mention the theoretical calculation that incomplete ionization occurs whenever there is parallel resonant excitation into a common level with ionization loss.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       We consider a state such as 6f of Xe which consists of 7 quasi-degnerate levels in the range of the laser bandwidth. These levels along with the continuum constitute a lambda system. The mechanism of the lambda type trapping is schematically presented in figure. At the rising part of the pulse (a) the excited state (with two degenerate levels 1 and 2) are not in multiphoton resonance with the ground state. The electron is ionized through multiphoton coupling with the continuum. As the intensity of the pulse is increased the excited state and the continuum are shifted in energy due to the Stark shift. At the peak of the pulse (b) the excited states go into multiphoton resonance with the ground state. As the intensity starts to decrease (c), the two state are coupled through continuum and the population is trapped in a coherent superposition of the two states. Under subsequent action of the same pulse, due to interference in the transition amplitudes of the lambda system, the field cannot ionize the population completely and a fraction of the population will be trapped in a coherent superposition of the quasi degenerate levels. According to this explanation the states with higher angular momentum- with more sublevels- would have a higher probability of trapping the population. In general the strength of the trapping will be determined by the strength of the two photon coupling between the quasi-degenerate levels via the continuum.In 1996, using the very stable laser and by minimizing the masking effects of the focal region expansion with increasing intensity, Talebpour et al.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       observed structures on the curves of singly charged ions of Xe, Kr and Ar. These structures were attributed to electron trapping in the strong laser field. A more unambiguous demonstration of population trapping has been reported by T. Morishita and C. D. Lin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MorishitaLin2013_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MorishitaLin2013-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Non-sequential_multiple_ionization">
        Non-sequential multiple ionization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Non-sequential multiple ionization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The phenomenon of non-sequential ionization (NSI) of atoms exposed to intense laser fields has been a subject of many theoretical and experimental studies since 1983. The pioneering work began with the observation of a “knee” structure on the Xe
       <sup>
        2+
       </sup>
       ion signal versus intensity curve by L’Huillier et al.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From the experimental point of view, the NS double ionization refers to processes which somehow enhance the rate of production of doubly charged ions by a huge factor at intensities below the saturation intensity of the singly charged ion. Many, on the other hand, prefer to define the NSI as a process by which two electrons are ionized nearly simultaneously. This definition implies that apart from the sequential channel
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle A+L-&gt;A^{+}+L-&gt;A^{++}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

             </mi>
             <mo>
              +
             </mo>
             <mi>
              L
             </mi>
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo>
             <mo>
              &gt;
             </mo>
             <msup>
              <mi>
               A
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mo>
                +
               </mo>
              </mrow>
             </msup>
             <mo>
              +
             </mo>
             <mi>
              L
             </mi>
             <mo>
              −
              <!-- − -->
             </mo>
             <mo>
              &gt;
             </mo>
             <msup>
              <mi>
               A
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mo>
                +
               </mo>
               <mo>
                +
               </mo>
              </mrow>
             </msup>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle A+L-&gt;A^{+}+L-&gt;A^{++}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>

       there is another channel
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle A+L-&gt;A^{++}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="A+L-&gt;A^{{++}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d0d752c3be0bd7853be29bcad6c50838239f4654" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:15.605ex; height:2.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       which is the main contribution to the production of doubly charged ions at lower intensities. The first observation of triple NSI in
       <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">
        argon
       </a>
       interacting with a 1
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9Cm" title="Μm">
        µm
       </a>
       laser was reported by Augst et al.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Later, systematically studying the NSI of all rare gas atoms, the quadruple NSI of Xe was observed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most important conclusion of this study was the observation of the following relation between the rate of NSI to any charge state and the rate of tunnel ionization (predicted by the ADK formula) to the previous charge states;
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>

            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\displaystyle W_{NS}(A^{n+})=\sum _{i=1}^{n-1}\alpha _{n}\left(\lambda \right)W_{ADK}\left(A^{i+}\right)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fdae5effc71cc4a2090b81563d3cd18089fdb453" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:37.298ex; height:7.343ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle W_{ADK}\left(A^{i+}\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

        </span>
        <img alt="{\displaystyle W_{ADK}\left(A^{i+}\right)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/10dd2e01cabc19d378be3f6df35df3c2fdd13f63" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:12.818ex; height:3.343ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the rate of quasi-static tunneling to i'th charge state and
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \alpha _{n}(\lambda )}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

              <mi>

               <!-- α -->
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>

               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              (
             </mo>
             <mi>

              <!-- λ -->
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">

             </mo>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \alpha _{n}(\lambda )}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\alpha _{n}(\lambda )" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/df714d6f38a604abbc8d9d1cbd845d0ce408f45f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:5.871ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       are some constants depending on the wavelength of the laser (but not on the pulse duration).
      </p>
      <p>
       Two models have been proposed to explain the non-sequential ionization; the shake-off model and electron re-scattering model. The shake-off (SO) model, first proposed by Fittinghoff et al.,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is adopted from the field of ionization of atoms by X rays and electron projectiles where the SO process is one of the major mechanisms responsible for the multiple ionization of atoms. The SO model describes the NS process as a mechanism where one electron is ionized by the laser field and the departure of this electron is so rapid that the remaining electrons do not have enough time to adjust themselves to the new energy states. Therefore, there is a certain probability that, after the ionization of the first electron, a second electron is excited to states with higher energy (shake-up) or even ionized (shake-off). We should mention that, until now, there has been no quantitative calculation based on the SO model, and the model is still qualitative.
      </p>
      <p>
       The electron rescattering model was independently developed by Kuchiev,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Schafer
       <i>
        et al
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Corkum,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Becker and Faisal
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeckerFaisal1996_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BeckerFaisal1996-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and Faisal and Becker.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The principal features of the model can be understood easily from Corkum's version. Corkum's model describes the NS ionization as a process whereby an electron is tunnel ionized. The electron then interacts with the laser field where it is accelerated away from the nuclear core. If the electron has been ionized at an appropriate phase of the field, it will pass by the position of the remaining ion half a cycle later, where it can free an additional electron by electron impact. Only half of the time the electron is released with the appropriate phase and the other half it never return to the nuclear core. The maximum kinetic energy that the returning electron can have is 3.17 times the ponderomotive potential (
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle U_{p}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                p
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle U_{p}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="U_{p}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5fdf01ea5c43d1e338d8b847556322c42a8c65a9" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:2.647ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       ) of the laser. Corkum's model places a cut-off limit on the minimum intensity (
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle U_{p}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                p
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle U_{p}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="U_{p}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5fdf01ea5c43d1e338d8b847556322c42a8c65a9" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:2.647ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       is proportional to intensity) where ionization due to re-scattering can occur.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kuchiev%27s_model.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="272" data-file-width="919" height="65" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Kuchiev%27s_model.png/220px-Kuchiev%27s_model.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Kuchiev%27s_model.png/330px-Kuchiev%27s_model.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Kuchiev%27s_model.png/440px-Kuchiev%27s_model.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kuchiev%27s_model.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Feynman diagram for the process of double ionization in an atom through re-scattering mechanism
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The re-scattering model in Kuchiev's version (Kuchiev's model) is quantum mechanical. The basic idea of the model is illustrated by Feynman diagrams in figure a. First both electrons are in the ground state of an atom. The lines marked a and b describe the corresponding atomic states. Then the electron a is ionized. The beginning of the ionization process is shown by the intersection with a sloped dashed line. where the MPI occurs. The propagation of the ionized electron in the laser field, during which it absorbs other photons (ATI), is shown by the full thick line. The collision of this electron with the parent atomic ion is shown by a vertical dotted line representing the Coulomb interaction between the electrons. The state marked with c describes the ion excitation to a discrete or continuum state. Figure b describes the exchange process. Kuchiev's model, contrary to Corkum's model, does not predict any threshold intensity for the occurrence of NS ionization.
      </p>
      <p>
       Kuciev did not include the Coulomb effects on the dynamics of the ionized electron. This resulted in the underestimation of the double ionization rate by a huge factor. Obviously, in the approach of Becker and Faisal (which is equivalent to Kuchiev's model in spirit), this drawback does not exist. In fact, their model is more exact and does not suffer from the large number of approximations made by Kuchiev. Their calculation results perfectly fit with the experimental results of Walker et al.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Becker and Faisal
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       have been able to fit the experimental results on the multiple NSI of rare gas atoms using their model. As a result the electron re-scattering can be taken as the main mechanism for the occurrence of the NSI process.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Multiphoton_ionization_of_inner-valence_electrons_and_fragmentation_of_polyatomic_molecules">
        Multiphoton ionization of inner-valence electrons and fragmentation of polyatomic molecules
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Multiphoton ionization of inner-valence electrons and fragmentation of polyatomic molecules">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The ionization of inner valance electrons are responsible for the fragmentation of polyatomic molecules in strong laser fields. According to a qualitative model
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the dissociation of the molecules occurs through a three-step mechanism:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        MPI of electrons from the inner orbitals of the molecule which results in a molecular ion in ro-vibrational levels of an excited electronic state;
       </li>
       <li>
        Rapid radiationless transition to the high-lying ro-vibrational levels of a lower electronic state; and
       </li>
       <li>
        Subsequent dissociation of the ion to different fragments through various fragmentation channels.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The short pulse induced molecular fragmentation may be used as an ion source for high performance mass spectroscopy. The selectivity provided by a short pulse based source is superior to that expected when using the conventional electron ionization based sources, in particular when the identification of optical isomers is required.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PengPuskas2012_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PengPuskas2012-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Kramers-Henneberger_frame_and_ionization_phase_effects">
        Kramers-Henneberger frame and ionization phase effects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Kramers-Henneberger frame and ionization phase effects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Studying the strong field ionization of the atom in so called Kramers-Henneberger (K-H) frame
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-henneberger_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-henneberger-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       leads to the conclusion that the ionization efficiency strongly depends on the temporal details of the ionizing pulse but not necessarily on the field strength and the total energy of the ionizing pulse pumped into the atom.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mathur_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mathur-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Kramers-Henneberger frame is the non-intertial frame moving with the free electron under the influence of the harmonic laser pulse. The free electron solution of the Newton equations for the electron in one dimension in the harmonic laser field

       <span class="mwe-math-element">

             <mn>
              0
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle x=0}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="x=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/953917eaf52f2e1baad54c8c9e3d6f9bb3710cdc" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.591ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       while for that initial condition the solution will be
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle x(t)=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              x
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              (
             </mo>
             <mi>
              t
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              )
             </mo>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              0
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle x(t)=0}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="x(t)=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/39d5a44241e71c6e25141d278126efa41a5de3dc" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:8.24ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       in the K-H and it will be therefore identical to the free electron solution in the laboratory frame. The electron velocity on the other hand is phase shifted both to the field strength and to the electron position:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {\mathrm {d} x}{\mathrm {d} t}}=-{\frac {F}{\omega }}\cos(\omega t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   d
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mi>
                  x
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mrow>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mi mathvariant="normal">
                   d
                  </mi>
                 </mrow>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mi>
                 F
                </mi>
                <mi>
                 ω
                 <!-- ω -->
                </mi>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               cos
              </mi>
              <mo>
               ⁡
               <!-- ⁡ -->
              </mo>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mi>
               ω
               <!-- ω -->
              </mi>
              <mi>
               t
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {\frac {\mathrm {d} x}{\mathrm {d} t}}=-{\frac {F}{\omega }}\cos(\omega t)}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\displaystyle {\frac {\mathrm {d} x}{\mathrm {d} t}}=-{\frac {F}{\omega }}\cos(\omega t)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f4242467dc3236837c1d4868d21d83404c4da42c" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:18.535ex; height:5.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Therefore considering the
       <a href="/wiki/Wavelet" title="Wavelet">
        wavelet
       </a>
       pulses and defining the ionization as the full escape from the line segment of the length 2r (or from the spherical region in three dimensions) the full ionization happens in the classical model after the time
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle r/(a\omega )}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              r
             </mi>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mo>
               /
              </mo>
             </mrow>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              (
             </mo>
             <mi>
              a
             </mi>
             <mi>
              ω
              <!-- ω -->
             </mi>
             <mo stretchy="false">
              )
             </mo>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle r/(a\omega )}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="r/(a\omega )" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/eca2a4b17a69ebe76b08ea9a0891883b1c7e3cd4" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.696ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
       </span>
       or no ionization at all depending if the harmonic field wavelet is cut at the zero minimum or the maximum velocity.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Dissociation_.E2.80.93_distinction">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dissociation_–_distinction">
        Dissociation – distinction
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ionization&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Dissociation – distinction">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       A substance may
       <a href="/wiki/Dissociation_(chemistry)" title="Dissociation (chemistry)">
        dissociate
       </a>
       without necessarily producing ions. As an example, the molecules of table sugar dissociate in water (sugar is dissolved) but exist as intact neutral entities. Another subtle event is the dissociation of
       <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">
        sodium chloride
       </a>
       (table salt) into sodium and chlorine ions. Although it may seem as a case of ionization, in reality the ions already exist within the crystal lattice. When salt is dissociated, its constituent ions are simply surrounded by water molecules and their effects are visible (e.g. the solution becomes
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrolytic" title="Electrolytic">
        electrolytic
       </a>
       ). However, no transfer or displacement of electrons occurs. Actually, the chemical synthesis of salt involves ionization. This is a chemical reaction.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">

       </span>

            </li>
